    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/20049625-chris"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1367716254i/20049625._UY100_CR4,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/20049625-chris">Chris</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Self-defeatism. You'll note that when it comes to his grades, he prefers to purposely fail his assignments rather than try his hardest and fail anyway<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Self-defeatism. You'll note that when it comes to his grades, he prefers to purposely fail his assignments rather than try his hardest and fail anyway. I assume his social life is the same; he'd rather make people hate him than try to get people to like him and be rejected anyway.<br /><br />Fortunately for him, Carla saw right through his tactics.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3993727116739714529"><b>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom, Louis Sachar</b><br/><br/>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom is a 1987 juvenile fiction book from the author Louis Sachar. The title comes from a point when a character, Jeff, accidentally enters the girls' bathroom while trying to go to the school counselor's office when a teacher gives him the wrong directions and is horribly embarrassed by it. <br/><br/>تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز سوم ماه سپتامبر سال 2007میلادی<br/><br/>عنوان: ته کلاس، ردیف آخر، صندلی آخر؛ نویسنده: لوئیس ساکر؛ مترجم: پروین </span>
  <span id="freeText3993727116739714529" style="display:none"><b>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom, Louis Sachar</b><br /><br />There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom is a 1987 juvenile fiction book from the author Louis Sachar. The title comes from a point when a character, Jeff, accidentally enters the girls' bathroom while trying to go to the school counselor's office when a teacher gives him the wrong directions and is horribly embarrassed by it. <br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز سوم ماه سپتامبر سال 2007میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: ته کلاس، ردیف آخر، صندلی آخر؛ نویسنده: لوئیس ساکر؛ مترجم: پروین علیپور؛ تهران، افق، 1385، در 262ص؛ شابک 9789643692681؛ موصوع داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی سده - 20م<br /><br />ته کلاس، ردیف آخر، صندلی آخر (پسری در حمام دختران) نام رمانی از «لوئیس سکر»؛ نویسندهٔ آمریکایی کتاب‌های کودکان است، که با عنوان اصلی در بالای این یادداشت در سال 1987میلادی، در آمریکا منتشر شد.؛ <br /><br /><b>هشدار: اگر داستان را میخواهید بخوانید، از خوانش ادامه ی این یادداشت خودداری کنید</b><br /><br />چکیده: «برادلی چاکرز» بزرگ‌ترین و خشن‌ترین شاگرد کلاس پنجم مدرسه بود، که همیشه در ته کلاس، ردیف آخر، روی صندلی آخر می‌نشست؛ او هیچ‌گاه دوستی نداشت، و هماره رفتارش ناشایست بود، و همه را عصبانی می‌کرد.؛ با این وجود که، پدر و مادر او دوستش می‌داشتند، اما او با آن‌ها نیز مشکل داشت.؛ «برادلی» از اینکه بدترین نمره را می‌گرفت، خوشحال می‌شد؛ و به هیچ چیز اهمیتی نمی‌داد، تا اینکه دانش‌آموز تازه ای به نام «جف فیشکین»، از «واشنگتن دی.سی.»، به آن مدرسه منتقل شد، و تلاش کرد، تا با «برادلی»، رابطه ی دوستانه برقرار کند؛ اما همانند دیگران در آن کار موفق نشد؛ مشاوری به نام «کارلا»، به مدرسه می‌آید، که در انتها، روند زندگی «برادلی»، با ورود این دو نفر به مدرسه، به کلی دگرگون می‌شود، و او روز به روز نمرات بهتری می‌گیرد، و همچنین با دیگران به خوبی کنار می‌آید؛ گرچه «کارلا» در آخر، به خاطر اعتراض‌های والدین، به روش‌های خاصش، در مشاوره با کودکان، مجبور می‌شود، از مدرسه برود؛ حال آنکه همهٔ دانش‌آموزان، با یاری او، بر ترس‌ها و مشکلات خود، پیروز شده بودند، و او را دوست می‌داشتند<br /><br />شخصیتهای داستان: برادلی چاکرز؛ جف فش کین؛ کارلا دیویس؛ کالین وریگولد؛ ملیندا بیچ؛ لری وستین؛ خانم چاکرز؛ آقای چاکرز؛ کلودیا چاکرز؛ بارتولومه (بارتولوموی)؛ رانی (رونی)؛ جودی؛ بتی؛ کارن؛ رابی؛ راسل؛ دن؛ براون؛ اندی؛ کوریس؛ داگ؛ شارن؛ خانم ویلکات؛ خانم کمپ؛ خانم وری گولد؛ آقای وری گولد؛ رییس هیات مدیره<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 01/09/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_3325111941" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3325111941" />
    <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt"><img alt="Rebecca McNutt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602390057p2/36977686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3325111941?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca McNutt" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt">Rebecca McNutt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=school">school</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3325111941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer150716521676410837">Well, I can honestly understand why <i>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom</i> was never hugely popular with readers. Its title borders on creepy, and the front cover artwork is visually unattractive. That said, a lot of this is misleading as to what the book is truly about, and the story itself isn't bad. <i>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom</i> deals with very important subject matter that a lot of kids simply don't understand. It follows Bradley, a social outcast who nobody seems to get along with or e</span>
  <span id="freeText150716521676410837" style="display:none">Well, I can honestly understand why <i>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom</i> was never hugely popular with readers. Its title borders on creepy, and the front cover artwork is visually unattractive. That said, a lot of this is misleading as to what the book is truly about, and the story itself isn't bad. <i>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom</i> deals with very important subject matter that a lot of kids simply don't understand. It follows Bradley, a social outcast who nobody seems to get along with or even like, and his struggles to change, with the help of a sympathetic school counsellor. <br /><br />While most public schools these days have anti-bullying programs and friendship-building activities, it goes without saying that public schools are also a breeding ground for stigmatizing anybody who is different, and most students are pitted against the same crowd for a full twelve years before they finally graduate. Louis Sachar captures exceptionally well the efforts of a social misfit with a bad reputation, and also the huge amount of difference that one caring person can make. Bradley is torn between genuinely wanting to change and wanting to make real friends, and holding up the troublemaker persona which has become his shield for most of his life.<br /><br /><i>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom</i> is one of those timeless books that definitely still belongs in classrooms for its thematic content alone, but also because Sachar truly understands how much it can suck to be a kid, and also the joy of overcoming that frustration. This book offers some hope that anybody, regardless of their reputation, has the capacity to be accepted and welcomed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="150716521676410837" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_595606333" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/595606333" />
    <a title="Julesmarie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6948666-julesmarie"><img alt="Julesmarie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334426054p2/6948666.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/595606333?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julesmarie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julesmarie" href="/user/show/6948666-julesmarie">Julesmarie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6948666-julesmarie?shelf=comfort-reads">comfort-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6948666-julesmarie?shelf=new-favorites">new-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6948666-julesmarie?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6948666-julesmarie?shelf=classroom-library">classroom-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer595606333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14392899653303639079">It's a shame the title is so ridiculous, because the book itself is not. It's an honest and insightful look in to what makes a person "good" or "bad" and how one changes the way others perceive him. It also shows beautifully the difference that adults can make on the lives and self-images of children--either to help them or to hurt them (hopefully unintentionally).<br/><br/>My strongest emotions at the end of this were anger and gratitude. The portrayal of the school system in this book is heartbreaking. </span>
  <span id="freeText14392899653303639079" style="display:none">It's a shame the title is so ridiculous, because the book itself is not. It's an honest and insightful look in to what makes a person "good" or "bad" and how one changes the way others perceive him. It also shows beautifully the difference that adults can make on the lives and self-images of children--either to help them or to hurt them (hopefully unintentionally).<br /><br />My strongest emotions at the end of this were anger and gratitude. The portrayal of the school system in this book is heartbreaking. Bradley's teacher frustrated me so much--people like that have no business being teachers. She had him sitting in a back corner surrounded by empty desks and told the new boy that she was sorry those were the only open seats, because she knows nobody wants to sit by THAT boy.<br /><br />And then announcing to the class that there were 16 A's and the rest B's... except for one F, when every student in the class will know who got the F is beyond belief heartless.<br /><br />What honestly made me angry was the depiction of the school board and the angry parent meeting that got Carla fired. The one person who actually cares about the students and helping them learn to think for themselves (educating them, in other words) is the person they gang up on and reject. The saddest part is that I fear that depiction is all too accurate in so many cases.<br /><br />What made me grateful was that in the midst of all of these horrible teachers and misguided systems, there are people like Carla. People who aren't so quick to give up on the students. People who want to help mold healthy individuals and not simply produce students who excel at doing what they're told. People who don't make the poor Mom have to repeat "But deep down inside he's really a good boy," because they can already see it for themselves.<br /><br />And the birthday party scene where all the girls start fighting over who gets to help Bradley and explain to him what happens at birthday parties goes a long way to restoring my faith in humanity after the school board scene. That whole party was just so wonderfully well done, with Bradley slowly relaxing and everyone being so kind to him and all of them just having so much fun as a result. Beautiful!<br /><br />Lovely, lovely book. I can't wait to recommend it to my students!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14392899653303639079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14030005342679113362">I really loved the way the intensity of Bradley was captured- and I went up and down with him as he made progress, then went backward, then forward again. There are so many beautiful touches: his absurdly long list of topics to talk about, his talking stuffed animals, the picture of the monster with the heart....<br/><br/>I was pleasantly suprised by this book, because the cover didn't indicate to me it would be so intense or layered. (The cover is a large, fairly realistic rendering of a surprised boy's </span>
  <span id="freeText14030005342679113362" style="display:none">I really loved the way the intensity of Bradley was captured- and I went up and down with him as he made progress, then went backward, then forward again. There are so many beautiful touches: his absurdly long list of topics to talk about, his talking stuffed animals, the picture of the monster with the heart....<br /><br />I was pleasantly suprised by this book, because the cover didn't indicate to me it would be so intense or layered. (The cover is a large, fairly realistic rendering of a surprised boy's face- and it looked like it would be light and maybe silly.) It had those layers without stepping out of being accessible to kids- which I think is pretty artful.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1593322673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11948913896899690492">Having blown through the Wayside books with my girls, I thought I'd pick up another Louis Sachar book. Holes is off limits - Liz and I have set some ground rules with what we allow ourselves to read out loud to the kids.<br/><br/>I saw this book and thought: WOAH. That's by Louis Sachar? No way! I'd always seen this lying around at home - in my sisters room. (A room covered in books.) I was also slightly embarrassed by the cover, which may help explain why I'd never read it.<br/><br/>Then, half-way through the book</span>
  <span id="freeText11948913896899690492" style="display:none">Having blown through the Wayside books with my girls, I thought I'd pick up another Louis Sachar book. Holes is off limits - Liz and I have set some ground rules with what we allow ourselves to read out loud to the kids.<br><br>I saw this book and thought: WOAH. That's by Louis Sachar? No way! I'd always seen this lying around at home - in my sisters room. (A room covered in books.) I was also slightly embarrassed by the cover, which may help explain why I'd never read it.<br><br>Then, half-way through the book, I realized I <i>HAD</i> read it. Not only that, but there were parts that stuck with me, and would bother me from time to time. I'd think about it, get a little angry about the scene, and then get angry with myself, because I couldn't remember where I had heard it. On more than one occasion I'd thought about asking about it in the goodreads "What's the Name of That Book?" group, but I never did.<br><br>In other news, it seems like boys bathrooms and girls bathrooms and girls and boys bathrooms and boys in girls bathrooms seems to be all anybody can talk about these days. States, cities, and localities are passing laws right and left. (For instance: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459199811i/18581465._SY540_.jpg" alt="transgender discrimination laws" class="gr-hostedUserImg">  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> In case you're wondering, reading this book had nothing to do with that.<br><br>As is the case when I read a book with any of my daughters, I'll let them come up and offer the true review. They also determine the number of stars to give the book. I read this one over the course of a month or so, at night, just before they all fell asleep - or were supposed to, at least. I'll go call them up.<br><br><b>There may be some mild spoilers in here. Read on at your own risk.</b><br><br>Dad: So, what'd you think?<br><br>Gwennie: I love, love, love, love, loved it!<br><br>El: I loved it, too!<br><br>Poppy: *scared* I don't know where to sit! *crams onto the bench*<br><br>*Just read what we have so far*<br><br>Poppy: Hey! I love, love, love, love, loved it TOO!!!<br><br>Dad: Poppy, what do you remember about the book?<br><br>Poppy: The. The. Uhhh... When he said, "GIVE ME A DOLLAR OR I WILL SPIT ON YOU!!!"<br><br>Dad: Did you like that part?<br><br>Poppy: Yessss.... Give me a dollar or I will spit on you! (She and Gwennie keep repeating this.)<br><br>Dad: Do you think it's nice to spit on people?<br><br>Poppy: No.<br><br>Dad: So why do you like that part?<br><br>Poppy: Uhhhhh... Because I just do.<br><br>Eleanor: Is it because it's interesting?<br><br>Poppy: Yes.<br><br>Dad: Eleanor, what's your favorite part?<br><br>Eleanor: I liked it when it was Colleen's birthday party! And my favorite character was Carla.<br><br>Dad: Why was Carla your favorite?<br><br>Eleanor: Um. Because she was really nice.<br><br>Dad: You were going to add some more stuff to that?<br><br>El: Yeah. I was going to add some more to my favorite part. I thought it was funny when Bradley tore his pants because he thought you had to have torn pants to go to a birthday party.<br><br>Dad: Gwen - your turn.<br><br>Gwennie: You mean my saddest favorite part?<br><br>Dad: Whatever you want. Go ahead. :)<br><br>Gwennie: <i>MY</i> saddest favorite part iiiisss... um... *looks around the room while chewing her hair* It was ... when Carla was leaving.<br><br>Dad: What do you mean?<br><br>Gwen: I mean, I didn't like it when Carla was going to go work with kindergartners.<br><br>Dad: Why not?<br><br>Gwen: Because it made Bradley sad.<br><br>Eleanor: I didn't like that part either, but I have a question.<br><br>Dad: What's that?<br><br>Eleanor: Why, when Bradley was going to the barbershop - and said he wanted to go to school - why did his mother- why was his mother like, "Bradley! Don't do that again!" He didn't do anything. I mean, I know that the car swerved, but I feel like it's not Bradley's fault that the car swerved. He just startled his mother a little. Why was she like that?<br><br>Dad: Well, Bradley's mother was driving the car, and holding on to the steering wheel. The way he startled her made her jerk the wheel - which made the car swerve.<br><br>El: But, he didn't mean to startle her, so why was she like that?<br><br>Dad: When you're in the car with a driver, you need to be careful to not startle, or distract the driver.<br><br>El: I get it. But I feel like when you're driving the car, and someone's crying really loud in the back seat we don't get in big trouble like that.<br><br>Dad: True. But, if I know the crying's going on - it's not the same kind of distracting as something that startles a driver.<br><br>Dad: Poppy, should we add anything else to the review?<br><br>Poppy: I have nowhere to sit. (She left to go look through some Halloween candy she found in here. No joke. It's March. And it's the day after Easter. And Poppy found some old Halloween candy.)<br><br>Gwen: I think I know what to add.<br><br>Dad: What are you going to add?<br><br>Gwen: I'm going to add that we liked the book.<br><br>El: AND I RECOMMEND IT TO PEOPLE! <b>OHHHH!!!</b> We forgot to say how many stars we're going to give the book!!!<br><br>Dad: How many stars are we going to give it?<br><br>El: Five! How many stars do you want to give it, Gwen?<br><br>Gwen: Um. F. I. V. E.<br><br>Poppy: (Has been saying five and six continuously)<br><br>Dad: Well, I guess it's decided then.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

    <a title="Michelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54041-michelle"><img alt="Michelle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316924020p2/54041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7628725?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle" href="/user/show/54041-michelle">Michelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394805726" href="/book/show/370565.There_s_A_Boy_in_the_Girls_Bathroom">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7628725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16977982896329379101">Review from April 2014<br/>I will never not love this book. Also, Louis Sachar forever.<br/>While I was rereading this book for the 10,000th time in my life, I was thinking that this book probably taught me a lot about empathy when I was younger. All kids should learn to have empathy. To put themselves in another person's shoes. To try to understand somebody's motivations. To be nice. <i>It's important.</i> The end.<br/><br/><br/>Review from October 2007<br/>I don't know why lately, but I've had problems committing myself to seriou</span>
  <span id="freeText16977982896329379101" style="display:none">Review from April 2014<br />I will never not love this book. Also, Louis Sachar forever.<br />While I was rereading this book for the 10,000th time in my life, I was thinking that this book probably taught me a lot about empathy when I was younger. All kids should learn to have empathy. To put themselves in another person's shoes. To try to understand somebody's motivations. To be nice. <i>It's important.</i> The end.<br /><br /><br />Review from October 2007<br />I don't know why lately, but I've had problems committing myself to serious, long, adult novels. I recently found this book at the Durham County Library Book Sale (best book sale EVER) and was ecstatic. I probably read this book 20 times when I was in the 1st, 2nd, and/or 3rd grade(s). It's about problem child, bully of the school Bradley Chalkers, and how he changes through friendship and believing in himself. It's a very sweet story that I'd recommend to anybody. Also, Louis Sachar, the author, is a genius. I love his other books that I've read, like Holes, and who can forget the Wayside School stories? Great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16977982896329379101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_177352775" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/177352775" />
    <a title="Ms. K" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5646208-ms-k"><img alt="Ms. K" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308279128p2/5646208.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/177352775?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms. K" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms. K" href="/user/show/5646208-ms-k">Ms. K</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394885704" href="/book/show/1475653.There_s_Boy_in_the_Girl_s_Bathroom">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5646208-ms-k?shelf=bullies-and-bully-ees">bullies-and-bully-ees</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer177352775" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer875146826447054540"> 	 This is a perfect read-aloud book, one I'd seen in classrooms for ages but never actually read until my coteacher suggested it. And she was right. There's so much to have deeper conversations around with our class.<br/><br/>Bradley, the 'bad kid', is everyone's idea of a nightmare, but the book slowly lets you in to see exactly how low his self-esteem is. I loved seeing Bradley alone in his room, using his model animals to allow himself to actually feel what he's feeling instead of just yelling ...more</span>
  <span id="freeText875146826447054540" style="display:none"> 	 This is a perfect read-aloud book, one I'd seen in classrooms for ages but never actually read until my coteacher suggested it. And she was right. There's so much to have deeper conversations around with our class.<br /><br />Bradley, the 'bad kid', is everyone's idea of a nightmare, but the book slowly lets you in to see exactly how low his self-esteem is. I loved seeing Bradley alone in his room, using his model animals to allow himself to actually feel what he's feeling instead of just yelling ...moreThis is a perfect read-aloud book, one I'd seen in classrooms for ages but never actually read until my coteacher suggested it. And she was right. There's so much to have deeper conversations around with our class.<br /><br />Bradley, the 'bad kid', is everyone's idea of a nightmare, but the book slowly lets you in to see exactly how low his self-esteem is. I loved seeing Bradley alone in his room, using his model animals to allow himself to actually feel what he's feeling instead of just yelling and raging and growling at everything around him to scare people off. In life it's hard to let people in and actually be yourself, especially when you hate yourself as much as Bradley does. He gets through it and realizes he can be a friend and a good person, but I love that it's slow and hard - it goes terribly over and over again. That's how it really is in life. You can't just fix stuff immediately.<br /><br />I also loved that Jeff - who at first I thought would be the main character who "gets to know the real Bradley" and understands him - was actually the secondary character. He was a friend to Bradley, but he didn't just stick by him or discover him. He got angry at him, he ditched him for the "cool kids"; it was a lot more like real life. I also felt his frustration - after trying to Bradley's frustration, I would've given up too. You can only do so much to be someone's friend, after all. Sometimes they have to do things on their own!<br /><br />I also really like that Louis Sachar used the idea of a "monster" to symbolize Bradley. We all kind of feel like monsters sometimes. I know I can feel scary and terrible and like no one would want to be around. We all have things we hate about ourselves, even though mine aren't tentacles or having four fingers. But it reminds me of the movie "Monsters, Inc." - the monsters are scary to some people, but darn, aren't they also awesome and kind of adorable? So Bradley - and the rest of us - can be those things, too. Probably. Eventually.</span>
  <a data-text-id="875146826447054540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer6328914724898955024">At first I thought this would be the usual story of the problem child in the classroom learning to be good and making friends but the story didn't take the straight path from A-B.<br/><br/>The character development was much stronger than I expected in such a short book and there was no easy attribution of black hats and white hats.<br/>Bradley's family was quite pleasant and not to blame for his behaviour and the teachers though obviously exasperated with him were also more than happy to respond to any improve</span>
  <span id="freeText6328914724898955024" style="display:none">At first I thought this would be the usual story of the problem child in the classroom learning to be good and making friends but the story didn't take the straight path from A-B.<br /><br />The character development was much stronger than I expected in such a short book and there was no easy attribution of black hats and white hats.<br />Bradley's family was quite pleasant and not to blame for his behaviour and the teachers though obviously exasperated with him were also more than happy to respond to any improvement he made.<br /><br />To be honest in the first few pages I had serious concerns about Bradley's mental health with him being so totally isolated from the outside world and communicating only with his toys.<br /><br />Although the story was obviously leading us to the 'new Bradley' the setbacks he suffered were realistic and peripheral story lines showed us that none of the children passed through life completely untroubled.<br /><br />The only niggle I have is that during the party scene the author seemed to be laying the foundation for further development of Jeff's character but nothing came of it.<br />I did wonder if book was aimed at too young an audience (or maybe at non-reading under-achievers like Bradley) because I felt that the story and the author's talents would have been better served if the story had been in a longer format.</span>

<div id="review_16187422" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16187422" />
    <a title="Dani Butler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/929214-dani-butler"><img alt="Dani Butler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203636036p2/929214.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16187422?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dani Butler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dani Butler" href="/user/show/929214-dani-butler">Dani Butler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">children (8-12), young at heart, troubled children</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/929214-dani-butler?shelf=childhood">childhood</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer16187422&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16187422" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14483495942538833123">I read this book after I was done with the Wayside series by the same author <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6569.Louis_Sachar' title='Louis Sachar' rel='nofollow noopener'> Louis Sachars</a>. So because I was use to the humor of the Wayside stories it was a complete shock that this book is more series. From what I remember, this book is about a trouble kid who has animal figures (or stuff animals?) as friends because he doesn't have any friends at school. I remember being really involved with the character and overall enjoying the book even if it wasn't hilarious. The one detail that sticks i</span>
  <span id="freeText14483495942538833123" style="display:none">I read this book after I was done with the Wayside series by the same author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6569.Louis_Sachar" title="Louis Sachar" rel="nofollow noopener"> Louis Sachars</a>. So because I was use to the humor of the Wayside stories it was a complete shock that this book is more series. From what I remember, this book is about a trouble kid who has animal figures (or stuff animals?) as friends because he doesn't have any friends at school. I remember being really involved with the character and overall enjoying the book even if it wasn't hilarious. The one detail that sticks in my mind (and warms my heart) is this kid's older sister buys him another animal figure because she accidently steps (and breaks) his favorite one that is rabbit. She yells at him about how the rabbit was on the ground and it was his fault that it got broken and how he wanted to point out that the rabbit was really in the desert but knew she wouldn't understand. I know a random detail about the book but it hit home for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14483495942538833123" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9486951871609590824" style="display:none">I love this book sooooo much I can read it a thousand times and I wont get tired of it! The first time I read it (well actually they read it to me) I was in the third grade and I loved it. I have 3 copies of it and I just absolutaly love it. My favorite character is Bradley Chalkers and he is the main one too. He is my favorite character because I think he is really funny and towards the end of the story he like starts getting to know himself more and discoveres secrets about himself that he had been hiding. I think my favorite parts of the story where when they never put a star besides his name and he was very bad behaved. In my opinion I dont understand why they named this book like its named because this only happens in a very small part of the book. Anyway I really love it and I love when he has to go to the counseling office because he needs a counselor to help him. Everytime I read this book I discover something different and exciting about it. This last time I read it I concentrated more on it and I read and understood parts that I hadnt before. So I love this book!!!! I recomend it ssoooooo much!</span>

<div id="review_2726096291" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2726096291" />
    <a title="Aravena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35781816-aravena"><img alt="Aravena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575905866p2/35781816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2726096291?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aravena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aravena" href="/user/show/35781816-aravena">Aravena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2726096291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16073443526988563394"><em>"They call him a monster, and everyone treats him like a monster, and after a while, he starts believing it himself."</em><br/><br/>As a 30-year old man (albeit with the taste, mentality, and body build of 12-year old), I suppose I have no business reading this book, which is clearly aimed at pre-teen readers. And yet, I believe there’s a lot of value you from reading this kind of story as an adult and re-visit the time when you’re at the protagonist’s age. <br/><br/>Louis Sachar’s <em>Holes</em> was the one of the most inventiv</span>
  <span id="freeText16073443526988563394" style="display:none"><em>"They call him a monster, and everyone treats him like a monster, and after a while, he starts believing it himself."</em><br /><br />As a 30-year old man (albeit with the taste, mentality, and body build of 12-year old), I suppose I have no business reading this book, which is clearly aimed at pre-teen readers. And yet, I believe there’s a lot of value you from reading this kind of story as an adult and re-visit the time when you’re at the protagonist’s age. <br /><br />Louis Sachar’s <em>Holes</em> was the one of the most inventive, witty, and poignant books I’ve ever read, so I was more than willing to read another book of his. While <em>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom</em> isn’t as good or unique as <em>Holes</em>, it’s stil a decent read that’s more meaningful and poignant than its title indicates. The language used is fairly simple and should be easy to understand for kids (as well as novice ESL students), while also brimming with playful mischievousness and boyish humor that Sachar is renowned for. <br /><br /><em>Jeff stood up. "I’ll be right back. I have to go to the bathroom."<br /><br />"Which one?" Bradley asked.<br /><br />"Boys’", said Jeff.<br /><br />"Oh,” said Bradley. “I’ll wait for you here then".</em><br /><br />It’s easy to sympathize with Bradley—yes, the kid that is hated by practically his entire school, constantly makes up stories and lies to others, and is pretty obsessed at seeing what the girls’ bathroom looks like. The narration of his headspace feels like a nostalgic trip to the past, when you make up the lists of <em>Things that Make You Look Cool</em> and <em>Things that Make You Look Lame</em> in your own mind, and act accordingly. It’s also when kids of opposite sex feel like the most disgusting and yet also the most interesting thing at the same time, and when you end up doing the opposite of what you really want for some stupid reason.<br /><br /><em>TaBitGB</em> isn’t an instant classic of its genre; it’s a bit to formulaic/predictable and the characters (especially the non-Bradley ones) aren’t nuanced enough. There’s a good amount of funny and moving moments though, as well as food for thought for kids, teachers, and parents alike. I think the most memorable part for me is when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Bradley, encouraged by his new school counselor, finally did his homework properly. But, he eventually ripped up his work before he even submit it—simply because it’s easier and much less intimidating to keep himself inside the box that others put him in. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><em>"You want to know what I think? I think you want to get good grades. I think that the only reason you want to fail is because you’re afraid to try. You’re afraid that if even you try, you’ll still fail."</em></span>
  <a data-text-id="16073443526988563394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Franzi Robinsôn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17023291-franzi-robins-n"><img alt="Franzi Robinsôn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364357266p2/17023291.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer12463585676073450160">I chose this book to read because I found it in my shelf collecting dust.   <span id="freeText12463585676073450160" style="display:none">I chose this book to read because I found it in my shelf collecting dust. I've read this book years and years ago. <br /><br />When I was in year 7 we had to do a novel study. At that time I HATED reading, it was my least favourite thing to do and I refused to read the book. The book wasn't "There's a boy in the girls' Bathroom" but it was from the same author Louis Sachar. The book was called "Holes". Like I have said in my previous reviews I usually judge the book by their cover, and the book "Holes" had a very boring title page... <br /><br />We had to read to page 20 in the first period and I refused and put up a hissy fit, I did not enjoy it at first and it bore me to death.<br /><br />Then at home I decided to continue to read it because I had nothing to do and I was very bored. So I continued to read it and read, and read and read until I had only about 10 pages left. Good things meaning Bradley behaves better and Bradley and Carla become a love interest. Bad things meaning the parents of Carla’s clients did not approve because of Carla’s age so Carla was forced to move and Bradley went through a mental break down. <br /><br />In the end everything turned out fine and Bradley made friends and got his grades up.<br /><br />My favourite character is Carla because she is true to everyone and herself, she helps Bradley see within himself who he is, and she is so cute!<br /><br />My favourite quote is what Bradley says “Give me a dollar or I will spit on you” I really like this quote because later on in the book he goes to the same boy (Jeff) and says “I will give you your dollar back if you will be my friend”.<br /><br />This book gave out a great message. 1.To not go in the girls bathroom if you are a boy and 2. to be kind and friendly and people will do the same to you<br /><br />This book made me think about how that there are some people out there with social problems who just cant seem to make friends easily so you must sometimes make the first move to introduce your self. Here i am, my best friend has some social problems but because i made the first move and introduced myself me and my best friend are still besties for 10 years now.<br /><br />I rate this book 4/5 because it was really good, gave out many good messages and was really enjoyable to read.<br /><br />I put this book under the category on my Blog Bingo Board under “A book with a male main character” because Bradley is the main character and he is a boy.<br /><br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_542566742" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542566742" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13012187-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348185636p2/13012187.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/542566742?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/13012187-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer542566742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2349440991046770108">Though he’s more well-known for “Wayside School”, I know Louis Sachar best for “There’s A Boy in the Girls’ Bathroom”.<br/><br/>This book…is a difficult one. I don’t hate it like I hated “Blubber”, but it gets VERY uncomfortable at certain points. Bradley's general social awkwardness tends to strike too much of a chord with me, his behavior makes me cringe before he turns it around, and if I went into how much I hated the subplot of the parents bitching about Carla Davis we'd be here forever.<br/><br/>But in spite </span>
  <span id="freeText2349440991046770108" style="display:none">Though he’s more well-known for “Wayside School”, I know Louis Sachar best for “There’s A Boy in the Girls’ Bathroom”.<br /><br />This book…is a difficult one. I don’t hate it like I hated “Blubber”, but it gets VERY uncomfortable at certain points. Bradley's general social awkwardness tends to strike too much of a chord with me, his behavior makes me cringe before he turns it around, and if I went into how much I hated the subplot of the parents bitching about Carla Davis we'd be here forever.<br /><br />But in spite of all that...it's a good book. Bradley starts out an irritating little punk who deserves all the derision he gets, but thanks to Carla Davis he slowly matures into a person others want to be around, even if no one buys it at first and the other kids keep treating him like crap. He doesn't give up, and Carla is always there to help him.<br /><br />The running theme of Bradley’s escapism using his collection of animals is well-executed, too. He goes to them when he needs comfort, he runs a romantic story between a rabbit and a bear, he kills the rabbit off when he’s in a bad mood only to bring her back when he feels better. The animals “act” as his personal cheerleaders. This makes the ending all the more heartwarming in a bittersweet way: He writes Carla a letter, and sends her Ronnie as a gift. While it’s a sad ending to her “romance” with Bartholemew the bear, it’s a sweet gesture. He even smiles as he’s getting ready to mail the letter.<br /><br />So overall, this isn’t one of my favorite books in the whole world, but it’s a good one. Overall it’s a story of development and redemption and the power of friendship with a bittersweet ending. Even with the plot point that made me rage, I didn’t mind reading this one again, and I like to imagine Bradley grew up to be one of the more popular kids in high school once he’d really grown up and gotten the hang of the whole social scene. And that he eventually met Carla again and they remained friends forever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2349440991046770108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/2784667-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer653724023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13147739145426517263">I downloaded this book without any idea of what it was about. I wasn't even sure of the genre. We can only assume it was in the sales or something, some offer or whatever!<br/><br/>I wanted a short book to get my count up and the name intrigued me, so I dipped in a little earlier in the day (yesterday, as it's now almost 2am), and read bits at work. Seeing as it IS, in fact, a kid's book, it was quick to get through when I managed to pick it up. <br/><br/>Despite being a kids book, it does actually centre around se</span>
  <span id="freeText13147739145426517263" style="display:none">I downloaded this book without any idea of what it was about. I wasn't even sure of the genre. We can only assume it was in the sales or something, some offer or whatever!<br /><br />I wanted a short book to get my count up and the name intrigued me, so I dipped in a little earlier in the day (yesterday, as it's now almost 2am), and read bits at work. Seeing as it IS, in fact, a kid's book, it was quick to get through when I managed to pick it up. <br /><br />Despite being a kids book, it does actually centre around serious themes that take part in the lives of children. Different taboos, relationships, gender differences (minutely, very basic), honesty and trust. There's a lot of development in the characters of the children and there's a good balance in the attitudes of adults and children. None of them are really 100% good or bad, although Carla does come close to good and his teacher is most certainly close to being very, very bad.<br /><br />Some actions and attitudes change very quickly and seemingly out of nowhere, but I suppose this can be partly excused due to the nature of children. One minute they're best friends, then they're not and so on. But the core aspect of the story is, I think, respect. Self respect and respect for others. It probably does work out a little more perfectly and easily than in real life, but this is a children's book and as an influential/educational tool, could really teach children a lot. And adults who have to deal with behavioural problems. <br /><br />A really charming, surprising and touching quick read. I genuinely feel I've come away with a better outlook and maybe even understanding. It gives you a few things to think about.</span>

          </span>

    <a title="Sarah Adamson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24040859-sarah-adamson"><img alt="Sarah Adamson" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

        <a title="Sarah Adamson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Adamson" href="/user/show/24040859-sarah-adamson">Sarah Adamson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780679805960" href="/book/show/236058.There_s_A_Boy_In_The_Girls_Bathroom">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24040859-sarah-adamson?shelf=elementary-school">elementary-school</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24040859-sarah-adamson?shelf=children-s">children-s</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2849127752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3335873400247924277">This was a tough one. It’s on my daughter’s summer reading list for going into 4th grade. I hadn’t read it so checked it out from the library.<br/>I anticipated it being about a transgender kid until I noticed the publication date. And then I was disappointed. I wanted the book to be dealing with a current real issue. <br/>I forced myself to read it.<br/><br/>Wow.<br/><br/>I’m not actually sure where to start.<br/>It started as the sort of book I don’t really like - there are mean kids and bad kids and some of them are full of to</span>
  <span id="freeText3335873400247924277" style="display:none">This was a tough one. It’s on my daughter’s summer reading list for going into 4th grade. I hadn’t read it so checked it out from the library.<br />I anticipated it being about a transgender kid until I noticed the publication date. And then I was disappointed. I wanted the book to be dealing with a current real issue. <br />I forced myself to read it.<br /><br />Wow.<br /><br />I’m not actually sure where to start.<br />It started as the sort of book I don’t really like - there are mean kids and bad kids and some of them are full of toilet humor and some are lovey-dovey seeing romance everywhere.<br /><br />Then Carla, the counselor entered the book. <br />Immediately this book changed from a classic school story about kids being the worst sort of kids to being a book about real kids, poking at each other and themselves to learn - about themselves and what they really wanted and like in life, about their friends and not-so friends to give them a chance to explore and learn who they are.<br /><br />This book gave me the best child psychology lessons I’ve had since medical school! <br /><br />Bradley is the brat, the kid who got held back by a year but is physically bigger and yet emotionally more immature. Jeff is the new kid who just wants to fit in. Carla is the new counselor, who comes with no agenda, no right answers, but is a guide to be there for whatever the kids need and to direct them to find their paths by themselves instead of giving them all the correct answers. <br /><br />I want Carla in my daughter’s life. I desperately want her in our school. <br /><br />Incredibly well written. <br /><br />I will read this with my daughter because I’m not sure she will get past the first school nonsense part to realize just how awesome this book is.<br /></span>

<div id="review_518752936" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/518752936" />
    <a title="Michael Mccarthy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10872684-michael-mccarthy"><img alt="Michael Mccarthy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/518752936?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Mccarthy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Mccarthy" href="/user/show/10872684-michael-mccarthy">Michael Mccarthy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer518752936" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18349021937920545491">Boy in the girls Bathroom <br/><br/>This story is about a boy called Bradley Chalker it is set in a school. Instead of doing his work Bradley prefers to cut up pieces of paper, or partake in other mindless tasks which keep his mind off the lesson. He initially wants everyone to hate him so they leave him alone. He sits at the back of the classroom last seat, last row. This behavior has led to his teachers and fellow classmate prefers to ignore him. When a new classmate, Jeff Fishkin, comes in from Washing</span>
  <span id="freeText18349021937920545491" style="display:none">Boy in the girls Bathroom <br /><br />This story is about a boy called Bradley Chalker it is set in a school. Instead of doing his work Bradley prefers to cut up pieces of paper, or partake in other mindless tasks which keep his mind off the lesson. He initially wants everyone to hate him so they leave him alone. He sits at the back of the classroom last seat, last row. This behavior has led to his teachers and fellow classmate prefers to ignore him. When a new classmate, Jeff Fishkin, comes in from Washington, DC, he has to sit next to Bradley because these are the only seats available in the classroom. Carla a counselor is appointed, and after a considerable amount of effort she beginnings to open him up. She loans Bradley a book for a book report and he thinks that the book is magical and responsible for his changes.<br /><br />Carla is not as popular with many parents of the students and is fired. Bradley becomes very upset and threatens to revert back to his bad behavior. In the end, Bradley starts to come to terms with Carla's departure. He writes her a goodbye letter sends his most prized possession with it, a small china rabbit named Ronnie.<br />I like this story because it directly relates to issues in the classroom that children can identify with. The plot is interesting and there is a positive but not all together happy ending. <br />This could effectively be used as a text to support the curriculum for example children can be asked about how Bradley feels at different stages of the book and create a feeling grid. <br />Age 9-11<br /></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WHXJRfgZtlynBwAnOzSKADGJcWvquU9OOv5gkM+I8SFJFcrz5x+m1OaMBESnt2ian8NYOV2XHO5TtY/nMsqZ1g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('pP6/imyRm5bWHOfGi0aCv4gK8XnPfwIyJjrT5SAtLm21nrw8c5eLHpeX46UXxWAlJkDYK3hRUZJPcTyS3W9Gmg==')})
  }
//]]>

          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6569.Louis_Sachar"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1595359600p3/6569.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6054642698437054850">Louis Sachar (pronounced Sacker), born March 20, 1954, is an American author of children's books.<br/><br/>Louis was born in East Meadow, New York, in 1954. When he was nine, he moved to Tustin, California. He went to college at the University of California at Berkeley and graduated in 1976, as an economics major. The next year, he wrote his first book, <b>Sideways Stories from Wayside School </b>. <br/><br/>He was working </span>
  <span id="freeText6054642698437054850" style="display:none">Louis Sachar (pronounced Sacker), born March 20, 1954, is an American author of children's books.<br /><br />Louis was born in East Meadow, New York, in 1954. When he was nine, he moved to Tustin, California. He went to college at the University of California at Berkeley and graduated in 1976, as an economics major. The next year, he wrote his first book, <b>Sideways Stories from Wayside School </b>. <br /><br />He was working at a sweater warehouse during the day and wrote at night. Almost a year later, he was fired from the job. He decided to go to law school. He attended Hastings College of the Law in San Francisco. <br /><br />His first book was published while he was in law school. He graduated in 1980. For the next eight years he worked part-time as a lawyer and continued to try to write children's books. Then his books started selling well enough so that he was able to quit practicing law. His wife's name is Carla. When he first met her, she was a counselor at an elementary school. She was the inspiration behind the counselor in <b>There's a Boy in the Girls' Bathroom </b>. He was married in 1985. Hisdaughter, Sherre, was born in 1987. <br /><br /></span>
